datasets. 3) Most of summary value for 2004 data is higher than 2019
data.

2)  Use the Date variable to create a new column for year, which will
    serve as an identifier.

``` r
library(lubridate)
```

    ## 
    ## Attaching package: 'lubridate'

    ## The following objects are masked from 'package:base':
    ## 
    ##     date, intersect, setdiff, union

``` r
Date <- as.Date.character(pm$Date, "%m/%d/%Y")
Year <- year(Date)
pm <- mutate(pm, Year)
head(pm)
```

3.  a). Create a basic map in leaflet() that shows the locations of the
    sites (make sure to use different colors for each year)

``` r
library(leaflet)
pal <- colorFactor(c('red', 'blue'), domain=pm$Year)
map <- leaflet(pm) %>%
  addProviderTiles('OpenStreetMap') %>%
  addCircles(lng=pm$SITE_LONGITUDE, lat=pm$SITE_LATITUDE, opacity=1, fillOpacity=1, radius = 50, color = ~pal(Year))
map
```

![](Homework-1_files/figure-gfm/unnamed-chunk-7-1.png)<!-- -->

b). Summarize the spatial distribution of the monitoring sites. From the
map, we can find that there are great number of sites gathering in Los
Angeles district and San Jose district. And the distribution range is
all over California.

4.  a). Check for any missing or implausible values of PM in the
    combined dataset. As we mentioned before, we have negative values
    for concentration in the dataset. In addition, the we need to find
    the implausible big value and NA value.

``` r
table(pm$PM2.5_Concentration)
```

``` r
mean(pm$PM2.5_Concentration)+3*sd(pm$PM2.5_Concentration)
```

    ## [1] 32.83465

It seems like no NA in this column. The normal range of value is 0-33.
b). Explore the proportions of each and provide a summary of any
temporal patterns you see in these observations.

``` r
negative <- pm[pm$PM2.5_Concentration <0]
proportion1 = nrow(negative)/nrow(pm)
proportion1
```

    ## [1] 0.003909434

``` r
bigvalue <- pm[pm$PM2.5_Concentration >33]
proportion2 = nrow(bigvalue)/nrow(pm)
proportion2
```

    ## [1] 0.02036221

From the calculation above, the proportions of negative value is
0.39%.It is a tiny proportion. But with big value, there is a larger
proportion, even with several very high values (above 100).

5.  Explore the main question of interest at three different spatial
    levels.Create exploratory plots (e.g. boxplots, histograms, line
    plots) and summary statistics that best suit each level of data. Be
    sure to write up explanations of what you observe in these data. For
    the convenience explore, we remove the negative value and value
    \>100.

``` r
pm_2004 <- pm_2004[pm_2004$`Daily Mean PM2.5 Concentration` >0]
pm_2004<- pm_2004[pm_2004$`Daily Mean PM2.5 Concentration`<=100]
pm_2019 <- pm_2019[pm_2019$`Daily Mean PM2.5 Concentration` >0]
pm_2019<- pm_2019[pm_2019$`Daily Mean PM2.5 Concentration`<=100]
pm <- pm[pm$PM2.5_Concentration >0]
pm<- pm[pm$PM2.5_Concentration<=100]
```

1)  state

``` r
boxplot(pm$PM2.5_Concentration~pm$Year)
```

![](Homework-1_files/figure-gfm/unnamed-chunk-11-1.png)<!-- -->

``` r
par(mfrow=c(2,1))
hist(subset(pm$PM2.5_Concentration, Year == 2004))
hist(subset(pm$PM2.5_Concentration, Year == 2019))
```

![](Homework-1_files/figure-gfm/unnamed-chunk-12-1.png)<!-- --> For
state level, 1) From the box plot, we can find the average level of
pm2.5 for 2004 is higher than 2019. The level has decreased over last 15
years. 2) From the histogram, more frequency of 0-20 concentration in
2019 than in 2004.2004 has more frequency of the higher level of pm2.5.

2)  county

``` r
county_04 <- aggregate(pm_2004$`Daily Mean PM2.5 Concentration`, by=list(type=pm_2004$COUNTY), mean)
county_19 <- aggregate(pm_2019$`Daily Mean PM2.5 Concentration`, by=list(type=pm_2019$COUNTY), mean)
```
